The Imam's Character and Virtues
Describes the Imam's piety, humility, and scholarly distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound impact of the Imam's association with Sufis and scholars of realities on his character, and how his piety, humility, and compassion made him an exemplary leader.

Core Idea
The Imam's character combined deep fear of God with unmatched humility and compassion for all.

This passage provides a vivid portrait of the Imam's spiritual and moral excellence. It highlights how his companionship with Sufis and scholars of realities refined his outward and inward states, resulting in a life marked by profound God-consciousness, humility, and service to others. As we read, note the integration of knowledge, action, and sainthood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is rich in descriptive language.
2
Identify key virtues
List the virtues mentioned: prolonged sorrow, much fear, humility, good character, tenderness, compassion, generosity, faithfulness, gentleness, respect for elders and young, humility before weak, reverence for prophethood.
Notice the progression from internal states to external actions.
3
Reflect on integration
Consider how the Imam combined knowledge, action, and sainthood. How did his compassion and preaching reflect his inner state?
The text says he was gathered for all three.

فكان في هذا إمام زمانه ، وقد أثرت فيه صحبة الصوفية وعلماء الحقائق ، فطاب خبره و مخبره ، وحَسُنت سيرته وسريرته كان رضي الله عنه طويل الحزن ، كثير الخوف ، ولشدة خوفه يسمع لصدره أنين و وهو مستغرق في الذكر ، فلا يشعر بمن معه ، مع تواضع وحسن خلق ورقة قلب ، رحيماً متبسماً في وجه من لقيه ، مع إقبال وحسن كلام ، يتزاحم الأطفال على تقبيل أطرافه ، ليناً هيناً حتى في مشيه ، ما ترى أحسن خلقاً ، ولا أوسع صدراً ، ولا أكرم نفساً ولا أعطف قلباً ، ولا أحفظ عهداً منه ؛ يوفّر الكبير ، ويقف مع الصغير ، ويتواضع للضعفاء ، مُعظَّماً جانب النبوة في غاية ، حتى ارتحل الناس إليه وتبرَّكوا به . لا يعارضه أحد إلا أفحمه ، جمع له العلم والعمل والولاية إلى النهاية ، مع شفقته على الخلق وقضاء حوائجهم عند السلطان ، وله الصبر على إذايتهم وضع من القبول والهيبة والإجلال في القلوب ما لم ينله غيره من علماء عصره وزُهّاده وكان جلُّ وعظه للناس في الخوف من المولى الجليل ومراقبته ، وذكر أحوال الآخرة ، وهو إلى هذا يعظ كل أحدٍ بما يناسب حاله ، وقل أن تراه إلا وهو يحرّك شفتيه بذكر الله تعالى(۱)
He was in this the Imam of his time, and the company of the Sufis and the scholars of realities had an effect on him, so his outward and inward became good, and his conduct and inner state became beautiful. He — may God be pleased with him — was of prolonged sorrow, much fear, and due to the intensity of his fear, a moaning would be heard from his chest while he was absorbed in dhikr, and he would not be aware of those with him, along with humility, good character, tenderness of heart, merciful, smiling in the face of whoever met him, with welcome and good speech. Children would crowd to kiss his limbs, gentle and easy even in his walking. You would not see anyone better in character, nor more broad-chested, nor more generous of soul, nor more compassionate of heart, nor more faithful to a covenant than him. He would honor the elderly and stand with the young, and humble himself before the weak, magnifying the side of prophethood to the utmost, until people traveled to him and sought blessings from him. No one would oppose him except he silenced him. He was gathered for knowledge, action, and sainthood to the end, along with his compassion for creation and fulfilling their needs before the sultan, and he had patience for their harm, and he attained from acceptance, awe, and veneration in hearts what none other of the scholars of his time and ascetics attained. And most of his preaching to people was about fear of the Majestic Lord and watching over Him, and mentioning the states of the Hereafter, and along with this he would preach to everyone according to what suited their state, and rarely would you see him except that he was moving his lips with the remembrance of God the Exalted.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a biographical description of the Imam's character and virtues. It begins by stating his standing as the Imam of his time, attributing his excellence to his association with Sufis and scholars of spiritual realities, which refined both his outward conduct and inner state. The description then details his emotional and spiritual states: he was habitually sorrowful and fearful of God, to the extent that his fear caused audible moans during dhikr, so absorbed that he was unaware of his surroundings. Despite this intensity, his character was marked by humility, good manners, tenderness, and a welcoming smile for all. Children loved him and would crowd to kiss his limbs, and he was gentle even in his gait. The text then lists superlative qualities: he had no equal in character, generosity, compassion, or faithfulness. He respected elders, stood with the young, and humbled himself before the weak, all while deeply revering prophethood. People traveled to him for blessings. In debates, he would silence any opponent. He combined knowledge, righteous action, and spiritual sainthood to the highest degree. His compassion extended to all, and he even interceded for people with the sultan. He patiently endured harm from others and was granted extraordinary acceptance and awe in hearts. His preaching focused on fear of God and the afterlife, but he tailored his counsel to each individual. A sign of his constant devotion was that he was frequently seen moving his lips in remembrance of God.

Misconception: Some may think the Imam was solely a scholar focused on academic pursuits.
Correction: The text emphasizes his spiritual piety, practical compassion, and intercession for people, showing that his scholarly authority was integrated with sainthood and service.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
He humbled himself before the weak and stood with the young. Think of the last time you interacted with someone vulnerable—did you lower yourself to serve them, or did you feel above them? Will you actively look for an opportunity today to humble yourself before someone weaker than you?

The Imam's Compassion for Animals and Children
Narrates three stories of the Imam's mercy towards animals and children.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Imam's piety manifested in his compassionate treatment of animals and children, reflecting his deep fear of Allah.

Core Idea
True piety shows mercy to every living creature.

This passage presents three vivid stories from the life of the Imam that reveal the practical outworking of his fear of Allah and his compassion. They show that his spiritual depth was not abstract but shaped how he walked, how he responded to harm done to animals, and how he instructed teachers. As you read, notice the link between his theological awareness (the soul as God's trust) and his ethical care.

Guided Path
1
The wolf and the soul
Read the first story about the wolf. Notice that the Imam wept and asked about the soul. What does this reveal about his view of animal life?
The soul is mentioned as a divine gift, even in a wolf.
2
Walking gently and protecting creatures
Read the Imam's advice to walk gently and look ahead. How does this simple instruction reflect a broader principle?
It shows constant awareness of potential harm to small creatures.
3
Mercy for animals and children
Read the two remaining parts: his reaction to animal cruelty and his prohibition of beating children. Notice the connection he makes between God's mercy and human mercy.
The one hundred mercies are for those who show compassion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن صور شفقته وخوفه من مولاه سبحانه : أنه مرَّ به ذئب يطارده صيَّادٌ وكلابُهُ فحبسوه ثم ذبح ، فلما وصل إليه ورآه ملقى على الأرض .. بكى وقال : لا إله إلا الله ، أين الروح التي يجري بها ؟!(۱) وكان يقول : ( ينبغي للإنسان أن يمشي برفق وينظر أمامه ؛ لئلا يقتل دابة في الأرض. وإذا رأى من يضرب دابة ضرباً عنيفاً .. تغيّر ، وقال لضاربها : ارفق يا مبارك ! وكان ينهى مؤدِّبي الكتاب عن ضرب الصبيان ، ويقول : ( الله تعالى مئة رحمة ، لا مطمع فيها إلا لمن اتسم برحمة وأشفق عليهم ((٢)
And among the forms of his compassion and fear of his Lord — glorified is He — is that a wolf passed by him being chased by a hunter and his dogs, so they captured it and then slaughtered it. When he reached it and saw it thrown on the ground, he wept and said: 'There is no god but God, where is the soul that flows through it?!' And he used to say: 'A person should walk gently and look ahead, lest he kill an animal on the ground.' And if he saw someone striking an animal violently, he would change expression and say to the striker: 'Be gentle, O blessed one!' And he would forbid teachers of children from beating the boys, saying: 'God the Exalted has one hundred mercies; there is no hope for them except for one who is characterized by mercy and has compassion on them.'
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a collection of three anecdotes illustrating the Imam's compassion (شفقة) and fear (خوف) of God. The first story narrates the Imam's encounter with a wolf that had been hunted and killed. He wept and exclaimed, 'There is no god but God, where is the soul that flows through it?!' This shows his profound reverence for life and his awareness of the soul as a divine trust, even in an animal. The second anecdote records his advice that a person should walk gently and watch their step to avoid harming any creature on the ground. The third describes his reaction to seeing someone strike an animal violently: his expression changed and he told the striker to be gentle, calling him 'blessed' (مبارك) as a gentle correction. Finally, he instructed teachers not to beat children, stating that God's one hundred mercies are only for those who are themselves merciful and compassionate. Together, these accounts demonstrate that his piety extended to the treatment of all vulnerable beings.

The Imam's Forgiveness, Charity, and Reflection
Relates the Imam's forgiveness of a wrongdoer, his charity, and his visits to ruins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three aspects of the Imam's character: his magnanimous forgiveness, his deep commitment to charity, and his practice of contemplative solitude.

Core Idea
True devotion combines forgiveness, generosity, and reflection on life's transience.

In this passage, we encounter three vignettes that reveal the Imam's inner character. They show him forgiving a wrongdoer at his deathbed, urging charity especially in hard times, and seeking moral lessons in ruined places. These practices reflect his focus on the afterlife and his detachment from worldly concerns.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the three distinct episodes described.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Reflect on the forgiveness incident
Consider the scholar who had wronged the Imam. The Imam forgave him and prayed for him. How does this reflect the Imam's character?
The scholar's reaction after the Imam's death shows the impact.
3
Examine the charity teaching
The Imam not only gave charity himself but commanded his family. He linked charity to love of Paradise, especially during hardship. Ask: why emphasize charity in times of high prices?
Generosity in difficulty is a sign of true faith.
4
Contemplate the visits to ruins
The Imam visited ruined places to reflect on mortality. Imagine standing where people once lived. What lesson is he teaching?
This practice cultivates detachment from the world.
5
Sum up the lesson
These three practices—forgiveness, charity, and reflection—form a model of spiritual life. Ask yourself how you can incorporate them.

وزاره في مرض موته بعض العلماء وكان قد أساء إليه ، فطلب منه السماح ، فغفر له ودعا له بالخير ، ولما مات الإمام بكى عليه هذا العالم وتألّم جداً وقال : فقدت الدنيا بفقده وكان يتصدَّق ويأمر أهله بالصدقة ، لا سيما أيام الشدة والجوع ، ويقول : ( من أحب الجنة فليكثر الصدقة خصوصاً في الغلاء ) (۳) . وكان يؤثر الخلوات ، ويزور المواضع الخربة للاعتبار ، ويقول : این سكَّانُها ؟ وكيف يتنعمون ؟
And a scholar visited him during his terminal illness, and he had done wrong to him, so he asked his forgiveness. He forgave him and prayed for good for him. And when the Imam died, this scholar wept over him and was very pained, saying: 'The world has been lost by his loss.' And he used to give charity and command his family to give charity, especially in times of hardship and hunger, and he would say: 'Whoever loves Paradise, let him give much charity, especially in times of high prices.' And he preferred solitude and would visit ruined places for reflection, saying: 'Where are its inhabitants? And how do they enjoy themselves?'
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage recounts three more aspects of the Imam's character: forgiveness, charity, and contemplative solitude. The first incident describes a scholar who had wronged the Imam visiting him during his final illness to ask for forgiveness. The Imam readily forgave him and prayed for his well-being. After the Imam's death, that same scholar wept bitterly and declared that the world had lost its value with his passing, showing the profound impact of the Imam's magnanimity. The second aspect is his emphasis on charity: he personally gave and instructed his family to give, especially during times of scarcity and hunger. He linked charity to love of Paradise, particularly during inflation. The third is his habit of seeking solitude and visiting ruined, desolate places for moral reflection. There he would ponder the transience of life by asking where the former inhabitants had gone and how they could enjoy themselves now. These practices demonstrate his detachment from worldly pleasures and his focus on the afterlife.

Mercy, Forgiveness, and Solitude: A Saint's Character
Anecdotes about the Imam's mercy, forgiveness, charity, and love of solitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Imam's character reflects deep mercy, forgiveness, charity, and a love for contemplation, and how these traits are rooted in Islamic spirituality.

Core Idea
Mercy and forgiveness are the hallmarks of a true believer.

The life of a scholar is not only about knowledge but also about embodying the teachings of Islam. The following anecdotes reveal the Imam's profound compassion for all creatures, his readiness to forgive, his generosity, and his habit of reflecting on the transient nature of this world. Through these stories, we see a living example of the Prophet's character.

صيَّادٌ وكلابُهُ فحبسوه ثم ذبح، فلما وصل إليه ورآه ملقى على الأرض بكى وقال: لا إله إلا الله، أين الروح التي يجري بها؟! وكان يقول: ينبغي للإنسان أن يمشي برفق وينظر أمامه؛ لئلا يقتل دابة في الأرض. وإذا رأى من يضرب دابة ضرباً عنيفاً تغير، وقال لضاربها: ارفق يا مبارك! وكان ينهى مؤدبي الكتاب عن ضرب الصبيان، ويقول: الله تعالى مئة رحمة، لا مطمع فيها إلا لمن اتسم برحمة وأشفق عليهم. وزاره في مرض موته بعض العلماء وكان قد أساء إليه، فطلب منه السماح، فغفر له ودعا له بالخير، ولما مات الإمام بكى عليه هذا العالم وتألم جداً وقال: فقدت الدنيا بفقده. وكان يتصدق ويأمر أهله بالصدقة، لا سيما أيام الشدة والجوع، ويقول: من أحب الجنة فليكثر الصدقة خصوصاً في الغلاء. وكان يؤثر الخلوات، ويزور المواضع الخربة للاعتبار، ويقول: أين سكانها؟ وكيف يتنعمون؟
A hunter and his dogs trapped it and then slaughtered it. When he reached it and saw it lying on the ground, he wept and said: 'There is no god but Allah, where is the soul that moves it?' Commentary
This passage is a collection of biographical anecdotes about the Imam's character. It first recounts his extreme compassion for animals: he wept upon seeing a slaughtered animal, reflecting his awareness of the soul's worth. Next, his charity is highlighted, especially during hardship, and he emphasizes charity as a means to Paradise. Finally, his love of solitude and contemplation of ruined places demonstrates his otherworldliness and constant reflection on the transience of life.

Anecdotes of the Imam's Asceticism and Worship
Anecdotes about the Imam's weeping, fasting, and extreme devotion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key anecdotes illustrating the Imam's profound asceticism, worship, and unique character traits.

Core Idea
True devotion weeps inwardly while fasting and serving others.

This passage shares intimate glimpses into the Imam's personal piety and character. From his constant weeping in fear of Allah to his extreme fasting and unparalleled modesty, these anecdotes paint a vivid picture of a saint who combined inner truth with outer asceticism. As you read, notice how his actions reflect the states of the true knowers (al-ʿārifīn).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage aloud or silently. Then review the literal translation to grasp the basic meaning of each anecdote.
2
Identify the key traits
As you read, highlight the Imam's qualities: his weeping, sleeping little, fasting, night vigil, modesty, and extreme frugality.
Notice the contrast between his inner state and outward behavior.
3
Reflect on the modesty incident
Pay special attention to the story about his shyness leading him to write intercessions for rulers, and his remarkable response about entering the Fire if shyness does so.
This shows how deeply ingrained his modesty was.
4
Consider the fasting details
The passage mentions the fast of Prophet Dawud (alternate-day fasting) and going days without food. Think about the level of detachment this represents.
Compare this with ordinary fasting practices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان يقول: كم من ضاحك مع الناس وقلبه يبكي خوف ربه فهذا شأن العارفين. وحاله مع الدنيا حال المسجون، فقل نومه وطال صومه، فكان ينام أول الليل ويحيي سائره، مع التزام الصوم عاماً إن هو رجع إلى النوم متى استيقظ، وهذا ما قد أثر في وجهه. وصدق فيه ما قيل: باطنه حقائق التوحيد، وظاهره زهد وتجريد، وكلامه هداية لكل مريد. وهو مع هذا كله شديد الحياء، حتى إنه كان يكتب الشفاعات للسلاطين حياءً ممن يسأله ذلك، فلامه على ذلك أخوه لأمه الشيخ علي التالوتي يوماً، فقال له الإمام: والله يا أخي؛ يمنعني منه غلبة الحياء، ولا أقدر أن أقول: لا أكتب، إذا كان الحياء يدخل صاحبه النار فأنا أدخلها. أحواله في يومه، وأثرة من أخلاقه. كان رحمه الله تعالى يصوم يوماً بيوم صوم سيدنا داود على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والسلام، ويفطر على يسير الطعام، ولا يبحث يوم فطره عما يأكل، وربما بقي ثلاثة أيام أو أزيد لا يأكل ولا يشرب، إن
He used to say: 'How many a one laughing with people while his heart weeps in fear of his Lord — this is the state of the knowers.' His state with the world was like that of a prisoner: his sleep was little, his fasting long. He would sleep at the beginning of the night and stay awake the rest of it, while observing continuous fasting if he returned to sleep whenever he woke up, and this had affected his face. It was truly said of him: 'His inner self contains the realities of tawhid, his outer self is asceticism and stripping away, and his speech is guidance for every seeker.' Despite all this, he was extremely shy, so much so that he would write letters of intercession for the rulers out of shyness toward the one who asked him. His maternal brother, Shaykh Ali al-Taluti, one day reproached him for that, and the Imam said to him: 'By Allah, O my brother, overwhelming shyness prevents me from it, and I am unable to say I will not write. If shyness lands its possessor in the Fire, then I will enter it.' His daily states and some of his virtues: He, may Allah have mercy on him, fasted every other day, the fast of our master Dawud (peace be upon our Prophet and upon him), and he broke his fast with little food, and he would not seek out what to eat on his day of breaking fast, and he might remain three days or more without eating or drinking, indeed
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage continues the Imam's biography, focusing on his asceticism and worship. It opens with his saying that true knowers (arifun) may laugh outwardly but cry inwardly out of fear of Allah. His relationship with the world is described as that of a prisoner: he slept little, fasted much, and maintained a night vigil. His practice of fasting every time he woke up (a form of continuous fasting) affected his appearance. The passage then praises him as embodying tawhid inwardly and asceticism outwardly, with his speech guiding seekers. A unique trait is his intense modesty (haya), which led him to write intercessory letters for rulers despite being shy; he even said he would enter the Fire if shyness does so. Finally, his daily routine includes the fast of Prophet Dawud (alternate-day fasting) and extreme frugality, sometimes going days without food or drink, showing complete detachment.

Key Takeaway
The Imam's life was a perfect blend of inner tawhid, outer asceticism, and extreme modesty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Are you so full of food and comfort that your face shows satisfaction with this world rather than longing for the next? Will you adopt a small act of fasting this week to train your soul?
?
The Imam wrote letters for rulers out of extreme shyness, saying even if shyness leads to the Fire, he would enter it. Is there any area in your life where you compromise due to shyness? Do you fear people's opinions more than Allah's displeasure? Will you repent and seek Allah's help to overcome false shyness?

Sanusi's Spiritual State: Inner Fear and Outer Asceticism
Biographical account of Sanusi's inner fear and outward asceticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual state of Imam Sanusi and how his outer cheerfulness coexisted with inner fear of Allah.

Core Idea
True knowledge of Allah produces both inner fear and outer cheerfulness.

This passage offers a glimpse into the personal spirituality of Imam Sanusi. Before we dive into his theological arguments, understanding his own spiritual state helps us appreciate the depth of his scholarship. The passage describes his ascetic practices and the contrast between his outward demeanor and inward reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the three distinct parts: a saying, a description of practice, and a testimonial.
2
Identify the three aspects
Identify the saying of Sanusi about the knowers (ʿārifīn), his personal practices (little sleep, long fasts), and the testimonial that summarizes his inner and outer state.
Each aspect reveals a layer of his spirituality.
3
Reflect on the inner-outer dynamic
Consider how Sanusi could be cheerful with people while his heart wept from fear of Allah. This is a key characteristic of the true knower.
This connects to the concept of zuhd (asceticism) not being gloomy but rooted in deep awareness.
4
Understand the terms
Note the key terms: ʿārif (knower), tawḥīd (divine unity), zuhd (asceticism), tajrīd (detachment), murīd (seeker). These are central to the passage and to kalam.
These terms will appear frequently in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان يقول : ( كم من ضاحك مع الناس وقلبه يبكي خوف ربه فهذا شأن العارفين ((۱) وحاله مع الدنيا حال المسجون ، فقل نومه وطال صومه ، فكان ينام أول الليل ويحيي سائره ، مع التزام الصوم عاماً إن هو رجع إلى النوم متى استيقظ ، وهذا ما قد أثر في وجهه وصدق فيه ما قيل : ( باطنه حقائق التوحيد ، وظاهره زهد وتجريد ، وكلامه هداية لكل مريد ((٢)
He used to say: 'How many a person laughs with the people while his heart weeps out of fear of his Lord; this is the state of the knowers (al-ʿārifīn).' His condition with the world was like that of a prisoner: his sleep was little and his fasting long. He would sleep at the beginning of the night and revive the rest of it, while committing to fasting for a whole year if he returned to sleep whenever he woke. This had an effect on his face, and the saying was verified concerning him: 'His inner is the realities of tawḥīd, his outer is asceticism and detachment, and his speech is guidance for every seeker (murīd).'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage describes Imam Sanusi's spiritual state and his detachment from worldly comforts. It begins with a saying he would utter about the true knower (ʿārif): outwardly cheerful but inwardly fearful of God. Sanusi himself lived like a prisoner in this world—sleeping little, fasting much, and waking at night for worship. He even committed to fasting a full year if he ever slept again after waking, showing extreme discipline. This lifestyle left a visible mark on his face. The passage concludes with a testimonial that his inner life was filled with the realities of divine unity (tawḥīd), his outer life was pure asceticism (zuhd) and detachment (tajrīd), and his speech guided seekers (murīdūn).

Key Terms
عارف
توحيد
زهد
تجريد
مريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that asceticism (zuhd) is forced or gloomy.
Correction: The passage shows that Sanusi’s asceticism was accompanied by outward cheerfulness and inner fear, not gloom. True asceticism is rooted in deep awareness of Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Sanusi say about the person who laughs with people?
A) He is a hypocrite.
B) His heart weeps out of fear of his Lord.
C) He is ignorant.
D) He is a true ascetic.

Key Takeaway
Imam Sanusi's life exemplifies the integration of deep inner knowledge of tawhid with outward ascetic detachment and joyful guidance of seekers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you laugh with people, what is the state of your heart? Do you hide tears of fear of Allah behind a cheerful face, or is your laughter a mask for heedlessness? This week, commit to checking your heart's state before and after social interactions.
?
Imam Sanusi lived like a prisoner in this world, sleeping little and fasting much. What worldly attachments have imprisoned your heart? Identify one attachment you can begin to loosen today and take a concrete step.
?
The saying 'His inner is the realities of tawhid, his outer is asceticism and detachment, his speech is guidance' was true for Sanusi. Which of these three is most lacking in your life? What specific action will you take this week to bring it closer?

Sanusi's Extreme Shyness: A Lesson in Human Nature
Anecdote of Sanusi's extreme shyness and his defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi's extreme shyness shaped his conduct and his unique perspective on this trait.

Core Idea
Shyness so strong it defies even the threat of Hell.

This lesson presents a personal anecdote from the life of Imam al-Sanusi, revealing a profound aspect of his character. Despite his scholarly authority, Sanusi was known for overwhelming shyness, which led him to write intercessions for rulers, an act his brother criticized. Through Sanusi's response, we glimpse his deep conviction about human nature and moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Pay attention to the situation: Sanusi writing intercessions for sultans, his brother's criticism, and Sanusi's defense.
Notice the phrase 'غلبة الحياء' (overpowering of shyness).
2
Analyze Sanusi's justification
Consider Sanusi's response to his brother. He says he cannot refuse due to shyness, even if it leads to Hell. What does this reveal about his understanding of his own nature?
He treats shyness as an involuntary trait, not a moral choice.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson
Think about why this anecdote is included in a theological work. It humanizes Sanusi and shows that even great scholars have personal struggles.
This builds a connection between the reader and the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو مع هذا كله شديد الحياء ، حتى إنه كان يكتب الشفاعات للسلاطين حياءً ممن يسأله ذلك ، فلامه على ذلك أخوه لأمه الشيخ علي التالوتي يوماً ، فقال له الإمام : والله يا أخي ؛ يمنعني منه غلبة الحياء ، ولا أقدر أن أقول : لا أكتب ، إذا كان الحياء يدخل صاحبه النار فأنا أدخلها (۳)
And despite all this, he was extremely shy, to the point that he used to write intercessions for the sultans out of shyness toward those who asked him for that. His maternal brother, Shaykh ʿAlī al-Tālūtī, once blamed him for that. The Imam said to him: 'By Allah, O my brother, it is the overpowering of shyness that prevents me from refusing, and I am not able to say "I will not write." If shyness leads its possessor into the Fire, then I will enter it.'
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage relates an incident illustrating Sanusi's extraordinary shyness. Despite his high spiritual station, he would write letters of intercession on behalf of people to sultans simply because he was too shy to refuse them. His half-brother, Shaykh ʿAlī al-Tālūtī, criticized him for this. Sanusi defended himself by explaining that his shyness overwhelmed him and he could not bring himself to say no. He even declared that if shyness were a sin that leads to Hell, he would accept that consequence rather than overcome his nature. This shows that his shyness was not a moral failing but a deeply ingrained trait he considered beyond his control.

Key Terms
حياء
شفاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi's writing intercessions for sultans indicates he was politically ambitious or weak-willed.
Correction: The passage explicitly states he did so out of overpowering shyness, not ambition, and he was willing to face Hell rather than overcome his shyness.

Checkpoint
Why did Sanusi write intercessions for sultans?
A) He wanted political power.
B) He was forced by the sultans.
C) He was too shy to refuse those who asked.
D) He hoped to gain wealth.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's extreme shyness was an involuntary trait that he accepted as part of his nature, even at the risk of divine punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area in your life where you let shyness or fear of others prevent you from doing what is right? What is the cost to your relationship with Allah?
?
Sanusi was willing to enter Hell rather than overcome his shyness. What trait in yourself do you cling to so strongly that you would rather face consequences than change it?

Life of Imam al-Sanusi

Sanusi's Fasting and Detachment
Sanusi's fasting and his response when asked about it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of Sanusi's asceticism as exemplified by his fasting pattern, his indifference to food, and his gentle response when questioned.

Core Idea
Sanusi's fasting was extreme yet balanced with humor and detachment.

Sanusi's daily practices reveal a profound detachment from worldly concerns. His fasting followed the model of Prophet David, but his attitude towards food and the playful exchange with his companions show a unique blend of discipline and gentleness. This passage introduces us to his ascetic approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on fasting
Read the Arabic sentence describing his fasting pattern and response to questions.
2
Reflect on the meaning
Consider why he would not specify whether he was fasting or not.
Notice his avoidance of self-identification with ritual states.
3
Connect to detachment
Think about how his response exemplifies detachment from both food and reputation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحواله في يومه، وأثرة من أخلاقه كان رحمه الله تعالى يصوم يوماً بيوم صوم سيدنا داود على نبينا وعليه الصلاة والسلام ، ويفطر على يسير الطعام ، ولا يبحث يوم فطره عمَّا يأكل ، وربما بقي ثلاثة أيام أو أزيد لا يأكل ولا يشرب ، إن أُتِيَ بطعام أكل ، وإلا بقي كذلك ، وربما سألوه بعد مضي جلَّ النهار : هل مفطر هو ؟ فيقول : لا مفطر ولا صائم ، فيقال له : لِمَ لا تعلمنا بفطرك ؟! فيبتسم . وربما مازح بعض أصحابه ، فلا تجد أحسن منه حينئذ ، ولا يرفع صوته ، بل يعتدل فيه ، ويصافح الناس ولا يمنع من قبل يده ، ولا يلبس لباساً مخصوصاً يعرف به ، بل ثوبه ما اعتاده الناس كان يكره الكلام بعد الصبح والعصر ، ويتراخى في صلاته بتكبيرة الإحرام بعد الإقامة ، ولا يكبر إلا بعد حين ، وكان بعد صلاة الصبح في مسجده يقرأ أوراده ، ثم يباشر بإقراء العلم إلى وقت الفطور المعتاد ، ثم يقف ساعة مع الناس على باب داره ، ثم يدخل ويصلي الضحى قدر قراءة عشرة أحزاب ، ثم يشتغل بالمطالعة نهاراً . وكان بعد الزوال يخرج لخلواته إلى الغروب ، وأحياناً يصلي الظهر مع الناس ثم يجلس يصلي ويتنفل ويقرأ إلى العصر ، ثم يشتغل بورده إلى الغروب ، فإذا صلى المغرب صلى ست ركعات ، وبقي في مسجده حتى يصلي العشاء ، ويقرأ ما تيسر ثم يرجع إلى داره وينام ساعة ، ثم يشتغل بالنظر أو النسخ ساعة ، ثم يصلي إلى طلوع الفجر ) فيا لها من أحوال شريفة ، نال صاحبها المقامات المنيفة ! فأكرم به
His states during his day, and some of his character traits. He, may Allah have mercy on him, used to fast one day and break one day like the fast of our master David (peace be upon him), and he broke his fast with little food, and he did not search on his day of breaking for what he would eat. Sometimes he would remain three days or more without eating or drinking. If food was brought to him, he ate; otherwise he remained like that. Sometimes they would ask him after most of the day had passed: 'Are you breaking the fast?' He would say: 'Neither breaking the fast nor fasting.' They would say to him: 'Why do you not inform us that you are breaking the fast?' He would smile. Sometimes he would jest with some of his companions, and you would not find anyone better than him at that time; he did not raise his voice but was moderate in it. He shook hands with people and did not prevent kissing his hand. He did not wear a particular garment by which he was known; rather his garment was what people customarily wore. He disliked talking after dawn and after afternoon prayer. He would delay in his prayer by pausing after the opening takbīr following the iqāma, and would not say the takbīr until after a while. After the dawn prayer he would recite his litanies in his mosque, then he would directly teach knowledge until the usual time of breakfast. Then he would stand for an hour with people at the door of his house, then he would enter and pray Ḍuḥā for the length of reciting ten ḥizbs. Then he would occupy himself with reading during the day. After noon he would go out for his seclusions until sunset, and sometimes he would pray Ẓuhr with the people, then sit praying supererogatory prayers and reciting until ʿAṣr. Then he would occupy himself with his litany until sunset. When he prayed Maghrib, he would pray six rakʿas, and remain in his mosque until he prayed ʿIshā’, and he recited whatever was easy. Then he would return to his house and sleep for an hour. Then he would occupy himself with reviewing or copying for an hour. Then he would pray until the break of dawn. What noble states! Their possessor attained lofty stations! How noble he is!
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed account of Imam Sanusi's daily practices and character. It opens with his fasting pattern: he followed the fast of Prophet David (Dāwūd), fasting one day and breaking the next. He ate very little when breaking, and was completely unconcerned with what food he would eat. Sometimes he would go without food or drink for three days or more, only eating if something was brought to him. When asked if he was breaking his fast later in the day, he would respond ambiguously—'neither breaking nor fasting'—and smile when pressed. This shows his detachment from worldly concerns and his gentle, humorous response. He also used to joke with his companions, yet always maintained moderation and a pleasant demeanor. He did not wear distinctive clothing, mixing with people normally. He disliked talking after Fajr and ʿAṣr (times of remembrance). In prayer, he would pause after the opening takbīr, reflecting mindfulness. His daily schedule was highly structured: after Fajr, he recited litanies then taught until breakfast; then socialized briefly; then prayed Ḍuḥā for an extended period (ten ḥizbs—about two and a half hours); then studied all day. After noon, he would spend time in seclusion until sunset, sometimes praying Ẓuhr with others then continued supererogatory prayers and recitation until ʿAṣr. After Maghrib, he prayed six rakʿas, remained in the mosque until ʿIshā’, then went home, slept for an hour, then spent an hour reviewing or copying texts, then prayed until dawn. The passage ends with praise for these noble states and the lofty stations he attained.

Key Terms
صوم داود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Asceticism requires rigid rules and no flexibility.
Correction: Sanusi's attitude shows that asceticism can include humor and ambiguity about ritual states.

Checkpoint
How did Sanusi respond when asked if he was breaking his fast late in the day?
A) He said 'Yes'
B) He said 'No'
C) He said 'Neither breaking nor fasting' and smiled
D) He ignored them

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's fasting exemplifies a balanced asceticism marked by detachment, flexibility, and gentle humor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi would go three days without food if nothing was brought to him. When was the last time you truly trusted Allah for your next meal, without any backup plan? Would you be willing to abandon your food planning and let Allah provide?
?
He avoided labeling himself as 'fasting' or 'not fasting' to escape the need for recognition. Is there any religious act you do that you secretly want others to notice? How can you practice that act in complete secrecy this week?
?
When pressed by others, Sanusi smiled instead of becoming defensive. Think of a recent moment when your spiritual practice was questioned. Did you react with irritation or with detachment? What would it take for you to respond with a smile like his?

Sanusi's Daily Worship and Teaching Routine
Sanusi's character and his daily schedule from Fajr to dawn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's structured daily schedule of worship, teaching, and seclusion, and how it reflects his devotion.

Core Idea
Sanusi's day was a seamless blend of worship, teaching, and personal devotion.

After describing his fasting, the passage moves to Sanusi's character and his meticulously organized day. From dawn until the next morning, his time was filled with litanies, teaching, prayer, seclusion, and study. This lesson explores that routine and its spiritual significance.

Guided Path
1
Read about his character traits
Read the part about his jesting, handshakes, and clothing.
Notice his humility and approachability.
2
Examine the daily schedule
Go through the timed activities from Fajr to dawn.
Pay attention to the sequence and durations.
3
Reflect on the balance
Consider how he balanced teaching, worship, and rest.

Source Text
